Invalid Conditions in Land Lease
Commentary on three conditions that invalidate a land lease

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions that invalidate a Hanafi land lease and explain why each leads to fasid, and compare the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the third condition.

Core Idea
Conditions leaving lasting benefit in leased land invalidate the contract.

This lesson examines three specific conditions that, when stipulated in a land lease, render the contract invalid (fasid) according to the Hanafi school. Understanding the rationale behind each condition—digging major rivers, spreading manure, and paying rent with cultivation of another land—is crucial for drafting valid ijarah contracts. We will also explore an internal Hanafi distinction between major rivers and small streams, and contrast the Hanafi stance with that of Imam al-Shafi'i on the third condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three invalid conditions
The commentary lists three conditions: (1) digging major rivers (al-anhār al-ʿiẓām), (2) spreading manure (al-sarqīn), and (3) paying rent by cultivating another land for the lessor. Each invalidates the lease under specific circumstances.
These are conditions in the lease contract itself, not separate contracts.
2
Understand the common rationale
All three conditions share the feature that the benefit provided to the lessor (improved irrigation, fertilized soil, or the crop from another land) persists after the lease term ends, creating an inequitable exchange akin to riba or gharar.
The Hanafi school treats benefits as fungible goods (aʿyān) in this context.
3
Distinguish major rivers from small streams
The commentary clarifies that small streams (jadāwil) are excepted because their benefit is consumed within the lease term. Digging them is already the lessee's duty, so the condition does not add lasting value.
This distinction is the preferred (sahih) position, though some Hanafi scholars equated all watercourses.
4
Analyze the Hanafi-Shafi‘i dispute on the third condition
Imam al-Shafi‘i considers the lease valid because he treats benefits as non-tangible and thus not subject to riba rules. The Hanafi school responds that benefits are analogous to aʿyān for the purpose of preventing riba, and the exchange without measure creates uncertainty (gharar).
The practical impact (thamarah al-khilaf) is that Hanafi jurists require monetary rent, while Shafi‘is permit the condition.
5
Apply the principle to analogous cases
The commentary extends the same ruling to renting an animal, house, or garment in exchange for the same type of benefit from the other party. All such exchange contracts are invalid if the benefits are of the same genus and unmeasured.
This is a qiyas khafi (subtle analogy) extending the land lease ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو يُسرقنها أو يزرعها، بزراعة أرض أخرى فسدت ذكر أن المراد الأنهار العظام ) ، فإنَّ منفعة كريها تبقى بعد انقضاء العقدِ بخلاف الجداول، (أو يُسَرْقِنَها ) (۳) ، فإنَّ منفعته تبقى بعد انقضاء العقد. (أو يزرعها ، بزراعة أرض أخرى فسدت) : أي استأجر أرضاً ليزرعها ، ويكون الأجرة أن يزرع المؤجر أرضاً أخرى هي للمستأجر، لا يجوز عندنا، وعند الشافعي ( يجوز أرضاً بشرط أن يحفر أنهارها العظام تفسد الإجارة ; لبقاء منفعته في العام القابل. [١] قوله : بخلاف الجداول ; فالإجارة بشرط كريها صحيحة; لأنه يجب على المستأجر بدون شرطه وإنه لا يبقى أثره بعد العام فلا تفسده، وهو الصحيح. قال الصدر الشهيد : واختاره الوالد برهان الأئمة ، وإليه ذهب في «الهداية»، وسوّى بعضهم بين الكبار والصغار في الفساد ; لإطلاق محمد ه ، وصححه في «الذخيرة»، واختاره خُواهر زاده له و به كان يفتي ، ذكره العلامة الحموي (٢) قوله : أو يُسرقنها ; عطف على قوله : يثنيها ; أي لو استأجر رجل أرضاً بشرط أن يضع فيها السرقين وهو الزبل ليتهيج الزرع، فالإجارة فاسدة، إلا إذا كان الريع لا يخرج من الأرض إلا بوضع السِّرقين فيها ، أو كانت المدة طويلة. كما صرحوا [٣] قوله : أو يزرعها ... الخ ; عطف على قوله : يثنيها أي لو استأجر رجل أرضاً بشرط أن يزرعها بزراعة أرض أخرى بأن جعلت زراعة الأرض الأخرى أجرة لها ، فهذه الإجارة فاسدة. وكذا استئجار دابة ليركبها بركوب دابة أخرى يركبها الآخر بمقابلتها. وكذا استئجار دار ليسكنها بسكنى دار أخرى ليسكنها الآخر بمقابلتها. وكذا استئجار ثوب ليلبسه بلبس ثوب آخر يلبسه الآخر بمقابلته. فالحاصل أن إجارة نوع بنوع آخر لا قدر فيهما فاسدة.
Or he spreads manure in it, or plants it with cultivation of another land, then it is invalid. He mentioned that what is meant are the major rivers, because the benefit of digging them remains after the expiry of the contract, unlike streams. Or he spreads manure in it, for its benefit remains after the expiry of the contract. Or he plants it with cultivation of another land, meaning he rented land to cultivate it, and the rent is that the lessor plants another land belonging to the lessee; this is not permissible according to us, but according to al-Shafi'i it is permissible. Land on condition that he dig its major rivers invalidates the lease because its benefit remains in the coming year. Commentary: Unlike streams, for a lease on condition of digging them is valid because it is obligatory on the lessee without the condition and its effect does not remain after the year, so it does not invalidate it, and this is the correct position. Al-Sadr al-Shahid said: and my father Burhan al-A'imma chose it, and he went for it in al-Hidaya; some equated large and small in invalidity due to the absolute statement of Muhammad, and it was declared correct in al-Dhakhira, and Khwahirzada chose it and used to give fatwa accordingly, mentioned by al-Hamawi. Commentary: 'Or he spreads manure in it' is conjoined to 'he digs it'; meaning if a man rents land on condition that he place manure (fertilizer) in it to stimulate the crop, the lease is invalid, unless the crop does not come out of the land except by placing manure in it, or the term is long, as they explicitly stated. Commentary: 'Or he plants it...' is conjoined to 'he digs it'; meaning if a man rents land on condition that he cultivate it with cultivation of another land, such that the cultivation of the other land is made the rent for it, then this lease is invalid. Similarly, renting an animal to ride it in exchange for riding another animal that the other rides in return. Similarly, renting a house to live in it in exchange for living in another house that the other lives in in return. Similarly, renting a garment to wear it in exchange for wearing another garment that the other wears in return. The conclusion is that renting one type for another type without a fixed measure is invalid.
Source pages p. 574

Commentary
This passage from the commentary addresses three specific conditions that, when stipulated in a land lease, render the contract invalid (fasid). The first condition is that the lessee must dig the major rivers on the land. The reasoning is that the benefit of digging (improved irrigation) persists after the lease term ends, which is akin to giving the lessor a permanent improvement without proper compensation. Small streams are excluded because their digging does not produce lasting benefit beyond the term, and the lessee is already obliged to maintain them. There is an internal Hanafi difference: some scholars equate large and small rivers in invalidity due to the general ruling of Imam Muhammad, but the preferred position (sahih) treats them differently. The second condition is spreading manure (sarqin) to fertilize the land. This invalidates the lease because the manure's benefit (enhanced soil fertility) remains after the term, unless the land cannot produce without it or the lease is long enough that the benefit is consumed. The third condition is that the rent is paid by cultivating another land for the lessor. This is invalid according to the Hanafi school, whereas Imam al-Shafi'i permits it. The Hanafi position is extended to similar exchanges: renting an animal, house, or garment in exchange for the same type of benefit from the other party. The principle is that exchanging benefits of the same genus without a fixed measure leads to uncertainty (gharar) and resembles riba, which is prohibited. The passage thus clarifies the boundary between valid and invalid conditions in ijarah contracts.

Key Terms
إجارة فاسدة (ijārah fāsidah): A lease contract that is defective due to an invalid condition, requiring correction or cancellation.
السرقين (al-sarqīn): Manure or fertilizer used to enrich soil for agriculture.
الأنهار العظام (al-anhār al-ʿiẓām): Major rivers that require significant digging effort, providing long-term benefit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a lease condition requiring the lessee to dig major rivers invalidate the contract?
A) Because digging is forbidden
B) Because the benefit of digging remains after the contract ends
C) Because the rivers belong to the government
D) Because the lessee is not capable of digging

Key Takeaway
Conditions that leave lasting benefit in leased land or involve exchanging like benefits without measure invalidate a Hanafi lease; small streams and necessary fertilizer are exceptions.

The Prohibition of Exchanging Benefits for Benefits: The Hanafi Rational Proof
Analogical proof for prohibiting benefit-for-benefit exchange

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi reasoning that equates benefit-for-benefit exchange to riba and apply the principle 'al-manafi' bi-manzilat al-a'yan' to evaluate contract validity.

Core Idea
Exchanging deferred benefits of the same kind is prohibited because it resembles a debt-for-debt transaction and thereby riba.

The Hanafi school rules that renting a house for another house (i.e., exchanging usufructs) is invalid. This ruling is not arbitrary; it is grounded in a rational analogy (qiyas) that treats benefits like tangible property (al-manafi' bi-manzilat al-a'yan). In this lesson, we will dissect the proof and see how Hanafi scholars refined the analogy against objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
The ruling is that exchanging benefits of the same genus (e.g., house for house) is fasid (invalid). The basis is the principle that benefits are treated like a'yan (fungible goods), and exchanging deferred benefits constitutes a prohibited debt-for-debt transaction (dayn bi-dayn).
This principle is unique to the Hanafi school.
2
Understand the analogy
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani equates the exchange to selling wheat for wheat on credit, which is riba al-nasi'ah. The effective cause (illa) is the delay and uncertainty in reciprocal delivery.
The analogy is implicit (qiyas khafi) and is supported by legal implication.
3
Examine the objections from al-'Inaya
Two objections are raised: (1) Riba requires a stipulated term, but here the delay is inherent; (2) In riba, one side is present and the other absent, but here both are absent.
These objections challenge the suitability of the analogy.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The response argues that inherent delay is even more problematic than a stipulated term, and by legal implication (dalala) the exchange is annexed to riba. The absence of both items creates a semblance (shubha) that warrants prohibition as a precaution.
The refinement shows a deep methodology: extending legal rulings through implication and precaution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنَّ المنافعَ بمَنْزِلَةِ الأعيان عنده [١] حتى جازت الإجارة بأجرة هي دين على المؤاجر، ولا يصير دينا بدين ، فلو لم تكن المنافع بمنزلة الأعيان لكان ذلك دينا بدين، وهو غير جائز. [١] قوله : ولنا... الخ ; حكى أن محمد بن سماعة الله أرسل كتاباً إلى محمد بن الحسن له يسأله : لِمَ لا يجوز إجارة سكنى دار بسكنى دار؟ فأجاب بقوله : إنك أطلت الفكرة، فأصابتك الحيرة ، وجالست الحنائي - اسم محدث - فكانت زلة عظيمة، أما علمت أن إجارة السكنى بالسكنى كبيع القوهى بالقوهـي نـسـيئة. ذكــره صاحب «النتائج»، وغيره. كذلك. قال في «العناية» (۲) : وفيه بحث من وجهين : الأول : إنّ النساء ما يكون اشتراط أجل في العقد وتأخير المنفعة فيما نحن فيه ليس وأما الثاني : هو إن النساء إنّما يتصوّر في مبادلة موجود في الحال بما ليس كذلك، فإن كل واحد منهما ليس بموجود هل يحدثان شيئاً فشيئاً. وأجيب . الأول : بأنهما لما أقدما على عقد يتأخّر المعقود عليه فيه، ويحدث عن شيئا فشيئاً كان ذلك أبلغ في وجوب التأخير من المشروط، فألحق به دلالة ; احتياطاً عن شبهة الحرمة. وفيه نظر; لأنّ في النساء شبهة الحرمة ، فبالالحاق به تكون شبهة الشبهة، وليست بمحرمة، والجواب : إنّ الثابت بالدلالةِ كالثابت بالعبارة، فبالالحاق تثبت الشبهة، لا شبهته حقا. وعن الثاني : بأن الذي لم تصحبه الباء يقام فيه العين مقام المنفعة ضرورة تحقق المعقود عليه دون ما تصحبه ; لفقدانهما فيه، ولزوم وجود أحدهما حكماً وعدم الآخر يحقق النساء. انتهى.
Because benefits are in the position of fungible goods (a'yan) according to him, such that a lease is permissible with a rent that is a debt on the lessor, and it does not become a debt for a debt. For if benefits were not like a'yan, that would be a debt for a debt, which is not permissible. Commentary: 'And for us...' It is narrated that Muhammad ibn Sama'a sent a letter to Muhammad ibn al-Hasan asking: Why is it not permissible to rent a house for a house? He answered: You prolonged thought, and confusion struck you, and you kept company with al-Hanai (the name of a traditionist), which was a great slip. Do you not know that renting a house for a house is like selling wheat for wheat on credit? Mentioned by the author of al-Nata'ij and others. Similarly. He said in al-'Inaya: There is a discussion from two perspectives: First, that riba is condition of a term in the contract and delay of the benefit, which is not the case here. Second, that riba is only conceived in exchange of something present now for something not present, whereas here each of the two is not existent now; they come into being gradually. It was answered as to the first: Because they have entered into a contract where the subject matter is delayed and comes into being gradually; this is more severe in necessitating delay than a stipulated term, so it is annexed to it by implication, as a precaution against the semblance of prohibition. But there is consideration: Because in riba there is a semblance of prohibition, and by annexation there is a semblance of a semblance, which is not prohibited. The answer: What is established by implication is like what is established by explicit text, so by annexation the semblance is established, not a semblance of it truly. As for the second: That which is not accompanied by the baa (i.e., immediate exchange), the 'ayn is set in place of the benefit out of necessity of realizing the subject matter, unlike that which is accompanied by it; because both are lacking in it, and the necessity of one existing in effect and the other not existing realizes riba. End.
Source pages p. 575

Commentary
This passage provides the rational proof (dalil aqli) for the Hanafi ruling that exchanging benefits (e.g., renting a house for a house) is invalid due to its similarity to riba. It begins by stating that benefits are treated like fungible goods (a'yan) in the law of contracts. This is evidenced by the fact that a lease can be valid with a rent that is a debt on the lessor (e.g., payment due later), and this does not become a 'debt for a debt' (which is prohibited). If benefits were not like a'yan, then this transaction would be a debt for a debt, which is not allowed. The commentary then recounts an anecdote where Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani responded to a question about renting a house for a house: he equated it to selling wheat for wheat on credit, which is explicitly prohibited riba. The passage then delves into a discussion from al-'Inaya, addressing two potential objections: (1) that riba requires a stipulated delay, whereas here the benefits are deferred by their nature, and (2) that riba applies to immediate exchange of absent items, whereas here both benefits are non-existent initially. The Hanafi response is that the natural deferred delivery is even more akin to riba than a stipulated delay, and by legal implication it is treated as riba. The second objection is refuted by stating that in non-immediate exchange, the 'ayn (actual item) is substituted for the benefit only by necessity; in this case the absence of both items creates the same uncertainty. Thus, the exchange is prohibited to avoid the semblance of riba.

Key Terms
المنافع بمَنْزِلَة الأعيان (al-manāfiʿ bi-manzilat al-aʿyān): Benefits legally treated like tangible assets for the purpose of contract rules, especially regarding riba.
دين بدين (dayn bi-dayn): A debt for a debt transaction, which is prohibited due to uncertainty and riba.
شبهة الحرمة (shubhat al-ḥurma): A legal semblance (suspicion) of prohibition that warrants avoidance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because houses cannot be rented
B) Because it is a debt for a debt since benefits are deferred
C) Because the rent must be monetary
D) Because the lessor and lessee are the same

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits exchanging benefits of the same genus because it resembles a debt-for-debt transaction and thereby riba, applying the principle that benefits are like tangible assets.

Like-for-Like Hire: Invalid Due to Riba al-Nasa'
Discusses like-for-like hire and valid conditions in land lease

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules like-for-like hire as fasid and distinguish it from valid hire contracts with differing benefits.

Core Idea
Exchanging the same benefit for itself with delay is invalid – it is riba al-nasa'.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses a key ruling: leasing a house for a house (like-for-like benefits) is invalid (fasid) because it involves riba al-nasa', just like selling wheat for wheat on credit. The commentary walks through the analogy, objections, and answers, then contrasts it with valid hire where the benefits differ or conditions are integral to the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is the exchange of similar benefits in a hire contract, e.g., leasing a house for a house. What is the ruling? The Hanafi school rules it fasid (invalid).
Fasid means defective; the contract is not void but produces some effects like ujrat al-mithl.
2
Locate the principal proof
Find the explicit statement from Imam Muhammad: 'Leasing a dwelling for a dwelling is like selling wheat for wheat on credit (نسيئة).' This is an analogy (qiyas) to riba al-nasa'.
The proof is not a direct text but qiyas; it is considered zanni in thubut and dalala.
3
Analyze the qiyas structure
Identify the asl (sale of wheat on credit), hukm of asl (invalid due to riba), far' (hire of same benefit), and 'illa (unity of genus and delay).
The 'illa is extracted by tahqiq al-manat: the genus (jins) and delay (nasa') are both present in like-for-like hire.
4
Examine objections and responses
Two objections are raised: (1) nasa' requires explicit term, but here delay is inherent; (2) nasa' requires one side present, but both benefits are non-existent. The responses hold that inherent delay is stronger, and in one side the building stands for benefit.
The first response uses dalalah (indication) to attach the ruling; the second uses the principle that the corporeal property substitutes for benefit.
5
Distinguish valid cases
Contrast with hiring land for plowing and sowing: that is valid because the benefits differ and conditions are required by the contract. When benefits are the same genus, there is no need to hire what one already has, so the original prohibition remains.
The passage also notes the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on compensation: Zahir al-Riwayah requires ujrat al-mithl; Abu Yusuf says nothing is due.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الإجارة / بـ الإجارة الفاسدة لأنَّ المنافعَ بمَنْزِلَةِ الأعيان عنده ولنا : أن الجنس بانفرادِهِ يحرِّمُ النَّسَاء عندنا قوله : لأن المنافع بمنزلة الأعيان عنده ؛ حتى جازت الإجارة بأجرة هي دين على المؤاجر، ولا يصير دينا بدين ، فلو لم تكن المنافع بمنزلة الأعيان لكان ذلك دينا بدين، وهو غير جائز. قوله : ولنا... الخ ; حكى أن محمد بن سماعة الله أرسل كتاباً إلى محمد بن الحسن له يسأله : لِمَ لا يجوز إجارة سكنى دار بسكنى دار؟ فأجاب بقوله : إنك أطلت الفكرة، فأصابتك الحيرة ، وجالست الحنائي - اسم محدث - فكانت زلة عظيمة، أما علمت أن إجارة السكنى بالسكنى كبيع القوهي بالقوهي نسيئة. ذكره صاحب «النتائج»، وغيره. كذلك. قال في «العناية» : وفيه بحث من وجهين : الأول : إنّ النساء ما يكون اشتراط أجل في العقد وتأخير المنفعة فيما نحن فيه ليس وأما الثاني : هو إن النساء إنّما يتصوّر في مبادلة موجود في الحال بما ليس كذلك، فإن كل واحد منهما ليس بموجود هل يحدثان شيئاً فشيئاً. وأجيب . الأول : بأنهما لما أقدما على عقد يتأخّر المعقود عليه فيه، ويحدث عن شيئا فشيئاً كان ذلك أبلغ في وجوب التأخير من المشروط، فألحق به دلالة ; احتياطاً عن شبهة الحرمة. وفيه نظر; لأنّ في النساء شبهة الحرمة ، فبالالحاق به تكون شبهة الشبهة، وليست بمحرمة، والجواب : إنّ الثابت بالدلالةِ كالثابت بالعبارة، فبالالحاق تثبت الشبهة، لا شبهته حقا. وعن الثاني : بأن الذي لم تصحبه الباء يقام فيه العين مقام المنفعة ضرورة تحقق المعقود عليه دون ما تصحبه ; لفقدانهما فيه، ولزوم وجود أحدهما حكماً وعدم الآخر يحقق النساء. انتهى. بخلاف استئجارها على أن يكربها ويزرعها، أو يسقيها ويزرعها، فإن لم يذكر زراعتها كبيع ثوب هروي بمثله، وأحدهما نسيئة. وقوله : فسدت جواب الشرط، وهو قوله : ولو دفع ... إلى آخره. (بخلاف استئجارها على أن يكربها ويزرعها، أو يسقيها ويزرعها)، فإنّه يصح; لأنَّ هذا شرط يقتضيه العقد، فإن لم يذكر زراعتها ولما كان يرد عليه الاشكالان، وإن أمكن الجواب عنهما، قال الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في «شرح الكنز» : الأولى أن يقال : إن الإجارة أجيزت على خلاف القياس للحاجة، ولا حاجة إلى استئجار المنفعة بجنسها ; لأنه يستغنى بما عنده منها فبقى على الأصل فلا يجوز. ولا كذلك عند اختلاف الجنس ; لأنّ حاجة كل واحد منهما إلى المنفعة التي ليست عنده باقية، ثم إذا استوفى أحدهما المنفعة عند اتحاد جنس المنفعة فعليه أجرُ المثل في ظاهر الرواية؛ لأنه استوفى منفعة بحكم عقد فاسد، فيلزم عليه أجر المثل، وروى الكرخي له عن أبي يوسف : إنه لا شيء عليه ; لأنه تقوم المنفعة بالتسمية، وقد فسدت كما ذكروا. قوله : كبيع ...الخ ; أي فصار كحكم بيع الثوب الهروي بمثله، وإنما لم يجز البيع هاهنا ; لأنّ أحد صفتي علة الربا كافٍ في حرمة النساء، وهو الجنس وقد وجد هاهنا. قوله : بخلاف...الخ ; أي بخلاف ما إذا استأجر رجل أرضاً على أن يكربها ويزرعها، أو يسقيها ويزرعها، فهذا الاستئجار جائز، والزراعة إنما تسخت بالعقد وهي لا تحصل إلا بالكراب والسقي، فاستحق كلّ واحدٍ منهما، وكل شرط يكون من مناسبات العقد يكون من مقتضيات العقد، فذكره غير موجب للفساد كما ذكرنا سابقاً.
Book of Hire / On Invalid Hire. Because benefits are in the status of corporeal property according to him. And for us: that the genus alone prohibits riba al-nasa' according to us. He said: because benefits are in the status of corporeal property according to him, such that hire is permitted for an wage that is a debt upon the hirer, and it does not become a debt for a debt. If benefits were not in the status of corporeal property, that would be debt for a debt, which is not permissible. He said: 'And for us...' etc. It is reported that Muhammad ibn Samā‘ah Allāh sent a letter to Muhammad ibn al-Hasan asking him: why is it not permissible to lease the dwelling of a house for the dwelling of a house? He replied: You have prolonged thought, so you have been afflicted with confusion, and you sat with al-Hanā'ī (a narrator's name), and it was a great slip. Do you not know that leasing a dwelling for a dwelling is like selling wheat for wheat on credit? Mentioned by the author of al-Natā’ij and others. Likewise. He said in al-‘Ināyah: In it there is discussion from two aspects: First: that riba al-nasa' is the stipulation of a term in the contract, and the delay of benefit in our case is not... Second: that riba al-nasa' is only conceived in the exchange of something present immediately for something not present. And in our case, each one is not present; they come into being little by little. And the answer to the first: because they entered into a contract in which the object of contract is delayed and comes into being little by little, so that is more extreme in the obligation of delay than the stipulated, so it is annexed to it by indication, as a precaution against the semblance of prohibition. But there is consideration: because in riba al-nasa' there is a semblance of prohibition, so by annexing to it, it becomes a semblance of a semblance, which is not prohibited. And the answer: that what is established by indication is like what is established by explicit wording, so by annexation, the semblance is established, not its semblance truly. And regarding the second: that that which is not accompanied by alif (the suffix) in it, the corporeal property takes the place of benefit out of necessity for the realization of the object of contract, unlike that which is accompanied by it, due to the lack of both in it, and the necessary existence of one of them in judgment and the absence of the other fulfills riba al-nasa'. End. Unlike hiring it (land) on condition that he plow it and sow it, or that he irrigate it and sow it, for if he does not mention its cultivation, it is like selling a Hirwī garment for its like, one of them being on credit. And his statement: 'it becomes invalid' is the answer to the condition, and it is his statement: 'and if he repays...' etc. (Unlike hiring it on condition that he plow it and sow it, or that he irrigate it and sow it), then it is valid, because this condition is required by the contract. For if he did not mention its cultivation and the two objections are raised, and it is possible to answer them, al-Zayla‘ī said in Sharh al-Kanz: The best approach is to say: hire is permitted contrary to analogy due to need, and there is no need to hire the benefit by its genus, because one can dispense with what one has of it, so it remains on the original principle and thus is not permissible. This is not so when the genus differs, because the need of each of them for the benefit that is not in his possession remains. Then if one of them receives the benefit when the genus of benefit is the same, then he must pay the fair wage (ujrat al-mithl) in the apparent riwāyah, because he received a benefit under a defective contract, so the fair wage is incumbent upon him. And al-Karkhī related from Abū Yūsuf that there is nothing required of him, because the benefit is valued by the specification, and it is defective as they mentioned. His statement: 'Like selling...' etc.; i.e., it is like the ruling of selling a Hirwī garment for its like, and the sale is not permitted here because one of the two attributes of the cause of ribā is sufficient for the prohibition of ribā al-nasa', namely the genus, and it has been found here. His statement: 'Unlike...' etc.; i.e., unlike if a man hires land on condition that he plow it and sow it, or that he irrigate it and sow it, then this hire is valid. And farming is only included in the contract, and it is not achieved except by plowing and irrigation, so each of them is entitled to it. And every condition that is among the correlates of the contract is from the requisites of the contract, so mentioning it is not a cause of invalidity as we have mentioned before.
Source pages p. 575

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āyah is a commentary on al-Wiqāyah dealing with invalid hire (al-ijārah al-fāsidah). The author first establishes the principle that benefits (manāfi‘) are treated like corporeal property (a‘yān) in the Hanafi school, which allows hire to be valid even if the wage is a debt. Then he addresses a specific question: why is it invalid to lease a house for a house (i.e., exchanging the benefit of dwelling for the benefit of another dwelling)? The answer is provided by Imam Muhammad: it is like selling wheat for wheat on credit (ribā al-nasā'), because the same genus of benefit is being exchanged with a delay, which is prohibited. The commentary then raises two objections against this analogy: first, that ribā al-nasā' normally requires an explicit stipulation of a term, while here the delay is inherent in the nature of benefits that come into existence gradually; second, that ribā al-nasā' involves exchanging something present for something absent, whereas both benefits here are non-existent at the contract. The answers to these objections are given: the first is rebutted by saying that the inherent delay is even stronger than a stipulated one, so it is attached by indication; the second is answered by noting that in one of the two benefits, the corporeal property (the house) takes the place of the benefit to ensure the object of contract exists in one party, thus realizing ribā al-nasā'. The passage then moves to a different scenario: hiring land with the condition that the tenant plows and sows it. This is valid because such conditions are required by the contract (muqtadayāt al-‘aqd). If the cultivation is not mentioned, the contract becomes analogous to the like-for-like exchange and is invalid. Al-Zayla‘ī explains that hire is permitted out of necessity, but there is no need to hire the same genus of benefit one already possesses. When the genus differs, the need remains, and the contract is valid. Finally, the compensation when a fasid hire is executed: in the apparent riwāyah, the hirer must pay the fair wage (ujrat al-mithl); a contrary riwāyah from Abu Yusuf holds that nothing is due because the benefit was valued by the invalid specification. This is a nuanced treatment combining proof from analogy and rational analysis.

Key Terms
الإجارة الفاسدة (al-ijārah al-fāsidah): A hire contract that is defective due to a condition that violates the contract's nature, such as exchanging like benefits with delay.
المنافع بمنزلة الأعيان (al-manāfi‘u bi-manzilati al-a‘yān): Benefits are legally treated like corporeal property in certain contexts, such as allowing hire and preventing debt-for-debt.
النساء (al-nasā'): Riba al-nasa' (delay usury) - prohibition due to deferment in exchange of same genus.
أجر المثل (ujrat al-mithl): Fair market wage paid for benefit received under a fasid contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, what is the ruling on leasing a house for a house (like-for-like benefit)?
A) Valid, because benefits are not subject to riba.
B) Valid, if the periods are equal.
C) Invalid, because it resembles selling wheat for wheat on credit (riba al-nasa').
D) Invalid, only if a term is explicitly stipulated.

Key Takeaway
Like-for-like exchange of benefits in a hire contract is invalid due to the prohibition of riba al-nasa', and if performed, the fair wage (ujrat al-mithl) is due.

Fasid Ijarah of Land Becoming Valid via Istihsan
Ruling on fasid ijarah of land with istihsan justification and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that a fasid lease of land for cultivation becomes valid after planting and expiry of the term, and distinguish the istihsan from the qiyas position within the school.

Core Idea
A fasid lease of land becomes valid after planting: istihsan overrides qiyas when uncertainty is resolved.

This passage addresses a common scenario in agricultural leases: hiring land without specifying the crop to be planted. According to the Hanafi school, such a contract is initially fasid (invalid) due to uncertainty (jahalah) concerning the object of the lease. However, the passage presents a notable application of istihsan (juristic preference): if the lessee goes ahead and plants the land, and the lease term expires, the contract becomes valid. This ruling, attributed to Abu Hanifa, departs from the strict qiyas (analogy) held by Imam Muhammad. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the conditions for generalization, and the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
First, identify the act: hiring land for cultivation without specifying the crop. The initial ruling according to the matn (base text) is that it is not valid (lam yasihh) unless the lessor generalizes permission. Recognize that this is a case of fasid ijarah due to jahalah (uncertainty).
The passage contrasts this with hiring a house, which always includes residence as a known benefit.
2
Locate the Dalil and Its Type
The proof for the validity after planting is istihsan, not direct text. Istihsan is a derived source (dalil 'aqli) that departs from qiyas when the latter leads to hardship or ignores the removal of the defect. Note that the istihsan is attributed to Abu Hanifa.
The matn states 'wa huwa istihsan' – and this is istihsan.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The cause (illa) for the initial invalidity is the uncertainty about what crop will be planted, which makes the object of the contract unknown. Once the lessee actually plants a crop and the term expires, this uncertainty is removed. Therefore, by istihsan, the contract becomes valid because the defect no longer exists.
The text explicitly says: the harm of cotton/rice vs wheat/barley differs, so specification is normally required.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
The passage indicates that Imam Muhammad held the qiyas position: the contract remains invalid even after planting, because the contract was initially defective. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Abu Hanifa's istihsan, as adopted by the authors of the later authoritative works.
The tarjih is based on the principle that removing the cause of invalidity after the fact can cure the contract in ijarah, similar to other contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو ما يزرع فيها لم يصح إن لم يعمّه، فإن زرعها ومضى الأجل عاد صحيحاً أو ما يزرع فيها لم يصح إن لم يعمه)، بأن قال : ازرع فيها ما شئت، وهذا بخلاف الدار ، فإن استئجارها يقع على السكنى على ما مرّ.
(فإن زرعها ومضى الأجلُ عاد صحيحاً)، وهو استحسان [١] قوله : لم يصح ... الخ ؛ عقد الإجارة ؛ لأنّ استئجار الأرض قد يكون للزراعة وقد يكون لغيرها من البناء، ونصب الخيم ونحوها.

انتفع

Or what is planted in it is not valid if he does not generalize it, then if he plants it and the term passes, it becomes valid. Or what is planted in it is not valid if he does not generalize it, meaning he says: 'Plant in it whatever you wish.' And this is unlike the house, for its hire includes residence as previously mentioned. (Then if he plants it and the term passes, it becomes valid.) And this is istihsan [1] His statement: '...is not valid' etc.; the lease contract; because hiring land may be for cultivation or for other purposes like building, erecting tents, etc. Likewise, what is planted in it varies; some causes less harm to it than others, for the harm of cotton and rice to it is greater than the harm of wheat and barley. Thus, specification is necessary at the time of contract; otherwise the object of contract would be unknown, leading to invalidity. This applies if the lessor does not generalize. But if he generalizes by saying: 'On the condition that you plant whatever you wish,' then it is valid due to his permission. The scholar al-Tahawi said in his hashia on al-Durr al-Mukhtar: 'Siraj al-Din narrated from al-Muhabbi that if one hires land for a known rent for a known period for resting and returning, if the meaning among people is that it is the same whether he benefits or not, then the lease is invalid because it dissolves into being stripped of benefit. Even if he explicitly stated that, it would be invalid, so likewise if he says for resting and returning. And if it means that he benefits from it with all benefits, then it is also a place of pause and consideration.' End. He supported it with what supports it remotely. And his statement: 'because it dissolves...' etc. is not apparent, because its meaning is by action or not, not that it is stripped of benefit. And his statement: 'And if it is...' etc. is also not apparent, because with generalization the lease is valid. End of what is in 'Hashiat al-Tahawi.'
Source pages p. 577

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific issue in invalid (fasid) leases: hiring agricultural land without specifying the crop to be planted. The ruling is that if the lessor does not allow the lessee to plant anything, the contract is invalid due to uncertainty in the object of the contract (crop type). However, if the lessor generalizes permission (e.g., 'plant whatever you wish'), the contract is valid because the lessor has waived the right to specify. The author then presents an istihsan (juridical preference): if the lessee plants the land and the lease term expires, the contract becomes valid. This is because the uncertainty is resolved once the actual planting occurs. The commentary clarifies that the difference in harm among crops necessitates specification, but once planting is done, the reason for invalidity (dispute) is removed. The Tahawi quote discusses a related scenario about hiring land for 'resting and returning' and concludes that it is invalid if it implies no benefit, but with generalization it may be valid. The commentator refines this by noting that the phrase 'it dissolves into being stripped of benefit' is not appropriate; rather, it means the lessee may or may not use it, not that benefit is denied.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): A legal preference that deviates from qiyas due to necessity or custom, used here to validate a lease after planting despite initial uncertainty.
جهالة (jahalah): Uncertainty or ignorance about a material term of the contract (here, the crop type), leading to invalidity.
فساد (fasad): Invalidity in a contract due to a defect in one of its essential elements, such as uncertainty in the object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) If the lessor specifies a particular crop at the start
B) If the lessee plants the land and the lease term expires
C) If the lessee pays a higher rent
D) If the land was previously used for construction

Key Takeaway
A fasid lease of land for cultivation becomes valid after the lessee plants and the term expires, based on Hanafi istihsan which removes the uncertainty.

Hiring a Camel Without Specifying Load
Ruling on liability when a hired camel dies from normal load in a fasid contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a hired camel dies under a fasid ijarah contract and explain the distinction between normal and abnormal loads, applying the principle of amanah.

Core Idea
In a fasid ijarah, the hired object is a trust; liability only arises from transgression.

This lesson explores a classic Hanafi case of an invalid lease agreement. When a camel is hired without specifying the load, the contract is technically invalid (fasid). Yet, if the camel dies while carrying a customary load, the Hanafi school holds the hirer not liable. Why? Because the camel becomes a trust in his hands, and liability only attaches to transgression. This ruling demonstrates the Hanafi principle of istihsan and the role of custom in determining contractual obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and its validity
Begin by recognizing that the contract is a lease of a camel for a journey (ijarah) but without specifying the load. This creates uncertainty (jahalah) in the object, rendering the contract fasid (invalid).
In Hanafi law, uncertainty in the essential terms of a lease makes it invalid, but not void ab initio.
2
Determine the liability rule
State the ruling: if the camel carries a normal load and dies, the hirer is not liable. The camel is considered an amanah (trust) in his possession.
The ruling is from the sound version (al-sahiha) narrated from Abu Hanifa.
3
Locate the proof: analogy to land lease
The text cites an analogy to Abu Hanifa's ruling on hiring land without specifying the crop. In that case, if the lessee plants and the term passes, he gets the specified rent, despite initial invalidity. Similarly, here the camel's loading of a normal load removes uncertainty.
The common illa is that performance (planting or loading) resolves the jahalah, and the object becomes a trust.
4
Distinguish normal vs abnormal load
Note that if the hirer loads an abnormal (excessive) load and the camel dies, he is liable because that is transgression (ta'addi). Custom defines what is normal.
Absolute permission (in a fasid contract) extends only to customary use.
5
Understand the underlying principle: amanah in fasid contracts
The key is that although the lease is invalid, it is 'legislated in one aspect', so the object is treated as a trust, not a guarantee. Liability arises only from transgression or negligence, not mere loss or death from normal use.
This is an application of istihsan: the fasid contract is given some effect to avoid injustice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ونصه : عن يعقوب له عن أبي حنيفة الله في الرجل يؤاجر الأرض ولم يسمّ أنه يزرع فيها شيئاً، قال: الإجارة فاسدة، فإن اختصما قبل أن يعمل فيها شيئاً أفسدتها ، وإن زرعها ومضى الأجلُ فله الأجر الذي سمّى. فتدبر. انتهى.

And whoever hires a camel to Egypt, and does not specify its load, and it carries the normal load and dies, he is not liable because the lease is invalid, so the property is a trust as in the sound version. And its text: from Ya'qub from Abu Hanifa regarding a man who hires land and does not specify what he will plant in it: He said: The lease is invalid. [2] His statement: 'before the completion of the contract' i.e., before the end of its term. [4] His statement: 'because the lease...' etc. Its summary: the property is a trust in the hand of the lessee, even if the lease is invalid, because invalidity is considered alongside validity, since it is legislated in one aspect.
Source pages p. 578

Commentary
This passage discusses a case of a fasid (invalid) ijarah contract: hiring a camel for a journey to Egypt without specifying the load. The main ruling is that if the camel dies while carrying a normal (customary) load, the hirer is not liable. The reason is that the contract is fasid, so the camel is held as an amanah (trust) in the hirer's possession, similar to a valid lease. The text then quotes Abu Hanifa's ruling on a related land lease case: if the lessee plants the land and the term expires, he gets the specified rent. The commentary clarifies that the uncertainty (jahalah) in the camel lease is removed by the actual loading of a normal load, as with the land case where planting resolves the uncertainty. However, the lessee is not liable for normal damage because there is no transgression. If he loads an abnormal load and the camel dies, he is liable. The commentator also addresses a point about the timing of annulment: the judge can annul the contract before the term ends, but if planting occurs, the uncertainty is gone and the contract stands. Overall, the principle is that in fasid ijarah, the object is an amanah, and the hirer is liable only for transgression (ta'addi) or negligence.

Key Terms
أمانة (amanah): A trust; the camel is considered a trust in the hands of the lessee, meaning he is not liable for loss unless due to negligence or transgression.
حمل معتاد (haml mu'tad): The customary load that is normally carried by such an animal; any load within that norm is allowed, and the hirer is not liable for harm to the animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In a fasid ijarah contract, the hired object is a trust; the hirer is liable only for transgression, not for loss from normal use.
